</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="Abs1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="Par1">Microgravity is associated with immunological dysfunction, though the mechanisms are poorly understood. Here, using single-cell analysis of human peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMCs) exposed to short term (25 hours) simulated microgravity, we characterize altered genes and pathways at basal and stimulated states with a Toll-like Receptor-7/8 agonist. We validate single-cell analysis by RNA sequencing and super-resolution microscopy, and against data from the Inspiration-4 (I4) mission, JAXA (Cell-Free Epigenome) mission, Twins study, and spleens from mice on the International Space Station. Overall, microgravity alters specific pathways for optimal immunity, including the cytoskeleton, interferon signaling, pyroptosis, temperature-shock, innate inflammation (e.g., Coronavirus pathogenesis pathway and IL-6 signaling), nuclear receptors, and sirtuin signaling. Microgravity directs monocyte inflammatory parameters, and impairs T cell and NK cell functionality. Using machine learning, we identify numerous compounds linking microgravity to immune cell transcription, and demonstrate that the flavonol, quercetin, can reverse most abnormal pathways. These results define immune cell alterations in microgravity, and provide opportunities for countermeasures to maintain normal immunity in space.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Subject terms:</strong> Adaptive immunity, Innate immunity, Gene regulation in immune cells, Space physics</p></section></section><section class="abstract" id="Abs2"><hr class="headless">
<p id="Par2">The phenotype and function of immune cells could change during spaceflight. Here the authors use simulated microgravity, coupled to validation with spaceflight data, to assess whether there are distinct gene expression changes in resting and TLR 7/8 stimulated PBMCs and found conserved changes in IFN signalling, the cytoskeleton, IL-6 and sirtuin signalling.</p></section><section id="Sec1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Introduction</h2>
<p id="Par3">Astronauts in low earth orbit (LEO), such as on the international space station (ISS), experience immune dysfunction associated with the microgravity environment. Multiple studies have described immune dysregulation in short or long-term simulated<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a>–<a href="#CR4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR4">4</a></sup> or actual microgravity<sup><a href="#CR5" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR5">5</a>–<a href="#CR9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR9">9</a></sup>. For the most part, such studies have described impaired T-cell responses, coupled with some form of heightened innate immunity<sup><a href="#CR7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR7">7</a>,<a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a></sup>, though some innate immune cells, like natural killer (NK) cells, also show impaired function<sup><a href="#CR11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR11">11</a></sup>. Consistent with altered adaptive immunity, potentially due to impaired cytotoxic and Th1 T cell function, and reduced NK cell function, astronauts develop increased reactivation of latent viruses, including herpes viruses (EBV, CMV, VZV)<sup><a href="#CR3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR3">3</a>,<a href="#CR7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR7">7</a>,<a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">12</a>–<a href="#CR15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR15">15</a></sup>. In one study, viral shedding after 9–14 days of spaceflight was linked to changes in serum cytokines, including a preferential large increase in IL-4 compared to interferon (IFN)γ, indicating a possible shift away from Th1 immunity towards Th2 immunity<sup><a href="#CR16" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR16">16</a></sup>. Consistently, some astronauts report heightened hypersensitivity reactions, such as increased allergic and Th2-like responses in space<sup><a href="#CR7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR7">7</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par4">Multiple studies using higher throughput approaches have started to add insight into pathways impacted by spaceflight. In the Twins study<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>, a one-year ISS mission altered innate, adaptive, and NK cell-mediated immunity across bulk RNA sequencing analysis. In T cells, increases in DNA methylation were seen in the promoters of <em>notch3</em> for CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells, linked to T cell differentiation, and in <em>scl1a5/asct2</em>, linked to activation, for CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells. A total of 50 of 62 assayed cytokines were also altered by spaceflight or landing<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>. During a recent multi-omic analysis, including bulk RNA and DNA methylation sequencing, of astronauts and mice in space, mouse organs such as the liver and kidney demonstrated reduced IFN signatures, coupled to altered methylation patterns of these gene sets, while muscles had increased IFNγ, IL-1, and TNF<sup><a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a></sup>. Serum inflammatory markers from 59 astronauts in this study (and in a similar companion study) showed increased VEGF-1, IGF-1, and IL-1 during spaceflight, which resolved upon returning to Earth<sup><a href="#CR4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR4">4</a>,<a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a></sup>. This same study also identified mitochondrial dysfunction as a major response of different non-hematolymphoid tissues to spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a></sup>. More recently, another study using a NASA-developed Rotating Wall Vessel, which was employed in our current work, utilized a 41-parameter mass cytometry approach to show that short-term (18–22 hours) simulated microgravity can dampen NK cell, CD4<sup>+</sup>, and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell responses to Concanavalin A/anti-CD28 stimulation, but potentiates STAT5 signaling to boost Tregs<sup><a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">18</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par5">Despite these important advances, the core fundamental mechanisms, genes, and pathways that are directly altered by microgravity to adversely impact immunity, including at single-cell resolution, are largely unknown. Interestingly, mechanical forces are emerging as critical orchestrators of immune cell function, whereby mechanotransduction tunes immune cell responsiveness to danger signals<sup><a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>. Some of these effects occur through environmental modulation of mechanosensing pathways that alter ion currents in cells, metabolism, or directly act on the cytoskeleton<sup><a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>. Thus, a spaceflight environment, which alters forces such as gravity, associated hydrostatic pressure, and shear force<sup><a href="#CR20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR20">20</a>,<a href="#CR21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR21">21</a></sup> onto immune cells likely directly contributes to immune system dysfunction.</p>
<p id="Par6">Here, using a common ground-based analog, the NASA developed low shear modeled microgravity Rotating Wall Vessel (RWV)<sup><a href="#CR2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR2">2</a>,<a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">18</a>,<a href="#CR22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR22">22</a></sup>, we examine in depth how short-term (25 hours) exposure to simulated microgravity impacts the human peripheral blood mononuclear immune system in detail at single-cell resolution. Combining this data with validation experiments from mice and crewmembers in LEO, as well as machine learning algorithms, we identify numerous core genes and pathways in immune cells that are altered by simulated microgravity or spaceflight, and identify numerous potential compounds that directly map onto immune cell transcriptional signatures in simulated microgravity.</p></section><section id="Sec2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Results</h2>
<section id="Sec3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Simulated microgravity alters the transcriptional landscape of individual immune cells</h3>
<p id="Par7">To begin understanding how simulated microgravity impacts immune cell function, we loaded PBMC samples from two young healthy CMV+ donors, one male, and one female, into either RWV simulated microgravity (uG) or normal gravity (1G) static controls for 16 hours of conditioning. The 16-hour-conditioning time point was chosen based on prior work that used approximately the same time and tracked transcriptional or proteomic changes on immune cells to simulated microgravity<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a>,<a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">18</a></sup>. PBMCs were either left unstimulated or stimulated for an additional 9 hours with R848, a standard TLR7/8 agonist. We chose TLR7/8 as a putative target since it mimics viral infection, and is expressed on most human immune cells, including T cells<sup><a href="#CR23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR23">23</a></sup>. Using this methodology, we next developed a single-cell atlas of 55,648 human PBMCs exposed to these conditions.</p>
<p id="Par8">In the unstimulated state, after 25 hours of simulated microgravity, we identified 28 clusters of immune cells visualized by UMAP (Uniform Manifold Approximation and Projection), including cell types such as mucosal associated invariant T cells (MAIT cells), double negative T cells, γδ T cells, innate lymphoid cells, and plasmacytoid dendritic cells, which have rarely been studied in simulated microgravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1A</a>). Simulated microgravity altered proportions of immune cell clusters to a mild extent, with B intermediate cells, and MAIT cell proportions being most negatively impacted, and CD14<sup>+</sup> monocytes, and CD4<sup>+</sup> T effector memory (TEM) cells being most increased based on percent change (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1B</a>). Across all immune populations, simulated microgravity altered expression of over 4500 genes with adj <em>P</em> cutoff of &lt;0.05 (Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM4" class="usa-link">1</a>). This list was refined to a core list of ~375 differentially expressed genes (DEGs) with an additional cutoff of |log2FC|&gt; 0.1. This list was further condensed to visualize on a Volcano plot with |log2FC|&gt; 0.25 (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1C</a>), showing only the very top positively and negatively altered genes. Volcano plots of DEGs for individual immune cell clusters are shown in Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>. Across all immune cells, some of the most induced genes in simulated microgravity included acute response genes such as <em>s100a8</em>, <em>s100a9</em>, <em>s100a12</em>, <em>thbs1</em>, heat-shock genes such <em>hsp90ab1</em>, chemokines like <em>ccl2</em>, <em>ccl4</em>, iron storage genes (<em>fth1</em>, <em>ftl</em>), and matrix metalloproteinases (<em>mmp9</em>). The most reduced genes in simulated microgravity included interferon response (<em>stat1</em>) and associated guanylate binding proteins (<em>gbp1</em>), and cold shock genes (<em>rbm3</em>, <em>cirbp</em>). Expression of the top DEGs (with mitochondrial encoded genes excluded for visual simplicity) across 22 populations of immune cells are shown in Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1D</a>. CD14<sup>+</sup> classical monocytes, CD16<sup>+</sup> nonclassical monocytes, and natural killer (NK) cells exhibited the most pronounced changes across major gene sets, consistent with short term simulated microgravity’s direct effect at reprogramming transcriptional changes most prominently in innate immunity. Consistently, using single-cell trajectory analysis, we identify numerous trajectories mainly in the innate immune cell clusters, especially the monocyte cluster, in response to simulated microgravity. Trajectory analysis is used to construct a path that describes how cells move through different states, and the numerous states seen in the monocyte cluster in simulated microgravity may reflect an increased capacity to generate distinct transcriptional states to simulated microgravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1E</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig1"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 1. Simulated microgravity without pattern recognition receptor stimulation alters the single-cell transcriptional landscape of human PBMCs.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig1_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/57c07a0daf09/41467_2023_42013_Fig1_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e805" height="858" width="800" alt="Fig. 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> UMAP plot of unstimulated PBMCs single-cell transcriptomes (10X Genomics), pooled together from a male (36 years old) and a female (25 years old) donor, that underwent either 1G or simulated microgravity (uG) for 25 hours total. Cells were resolved into 28 distinct clusters. <strong>B</strong> Quantification of relative abundance of each cluster of single PBMCs by percentage, or log2Fold Change (FC) between simulated uG and 1G conditions. Source data are provided with this paper. <strong>C</strong> Volcano plot of differentially expressed genes (DEGs) across all immune cell types between uG and 1G; DEGs (including log2FC and adj. <em>p</em>) were calculated by the MAST method. The Benjamini–Hochberg (B-H) method was used for multiple comparison adjustments. Adjusted <em>p</em> value (adj. <em>p</em>) cutoff is 0.05, and log2FC cutoff is 0.25. <strong>D</strong> Dot plot showing the top DEGs from <strong>C</strong> and their expression levels across 22 immune cell populations. DEGs (including log2FC and adj. <em>p</em>) were calculated by the MAST method. <em>P</em> values were adjusted by the B-H method. Spot color reflects Log2FC of uG vs 1G, while spot size shows −log10 (adj. <em>p</em>). <strong>E</strong> UMAP of trajectory analysis of 1G and simulated uG unstimulated PBMCs. White circles represent the root nodes of the trajectory. Black circles indicate branch nodes, where cells can travel to a variety of outcomes. Light gray circles designate different trajectory outcomes. <strong>F</strong> Canonical pathway enrichment analysis obtained from Ingenuity Pathway Analysis (IPA) is shown across 19 immune cell clusters. Spot color reflects IPA z-score enrichment of simulated uG vs 1G, with red meaning predicted activation of the pathway in uG and blue meaning repression of the pathway in uG. Spot size shows the level of significance via −log10 (adj. <em>p</em>). Adj. <em>p</em> was calculated by the Fisher’s Exact Test (right-tailed) followed by B-H adjustment.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par9">Ingenuity pathway analysis (IPA) (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1F</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM5" class="usa-link">2</a>) generated using our core list of 375 genes from the overall populations, as well as the DEGs in major immune cell types (Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM6" class="usa-link">3</a>) revealed that monocytes, conventional dendritic cells type 2 (cDC2)s, double negative (dn)T cells and NK cells show the most notable pathway alterations. Major pathways altered by simulated microgravity across immune cells included reductions in oxidative phosphorylation, interferon signaling like protein kinase R (PKR) in interferon response, nuclear receptor signaling (LXR/RXR, PPAR, AHR), RHOA and pyroptosis signaling, as well as increases in BAG2 (heat-shock protein 70 interactor) signaling, fibrosis signaling, actin-based motility, RAC, HIF1 signaling, acute phase response, oxidative stress and sirtuin signaling, amongst others.</p>
<p id="Par10">Given that multiple pathways we detected were associated with inflammatory processes linked to aging (i.e., increased innate immunity coupled to reduced adaptive immunity), we next determined whether acute exposure to simulated microgravity mimicked inflammatory aging processes in immune cells. We mapped the gene expression signatures of individual immune cells, and overall immune signatures, against two recently developed inflammatory signatures of aging, the inflammatory age (iAge) clock<sup><a href="#CR24" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR24">24</a></sup>, and the SenMayo list of senescence associated secretory inflammatory products<sup><a href="#CR25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR25">25</a></sup>. Simulated microgravity induced a significant enrichment in inflammaging related genes, consistent with the notion that short term simulated microgravity can induce aging-like inflammatory changes in unstimulated immune cells (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2A, B</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">2A</a>). Next, because both spaceflight and aging are associated with reactivation of latent viruses, we mined the meta-transcriptome of our single-cell analysis with meta-transcriptome detector (MTD) pipeline<sup><a href="#CR26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR26">26</a></sup>. Surprisingly, we saw that as little as 25 hours of simulated microgravity could induce the transcription of latent retroviruses and mycobacteria within human immune cells (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2C</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">2B, C</a> and <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">3</a>), directly implicating microgravity itself as a contributing trigger for latent pathogen activation. We confirmed the meta-transcriptome results with a different alignment tool, and we could still detect increases in <em>Gammaretrovirus</em> and <em>Mycobacterium canettii</em> transcripts seen with MTD pipeline (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">2B, C</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig2"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 2. Simulated microgravity induces predictive functional alterations in immune cells.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig2_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/4844f7acc3b6/41467_2023_42013_Fig2_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e946" height="349" width="681" alt="Fig. 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> Differences in iAge index between all cell types (left) and across 22 individual immune cell types (right) at 1G or simulated uG. Each box spans from the 25th to 75th percentiles (interquartile range, IQR), and features a median value denoted by a horizontal line. The whiskers extend to values within 1.5 times the IQR range from the 25th and 75th percentiles. 1G group (<em>n</em> = 11,934 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) is shown in blue and uG group (<em>n</em> = 16,568 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) is shown in yellow. The median for 1G group is 9.1, with min = −5.8 and max = 22.4. The median for uG group is 12.5, with min = −1.1 and max = 26.2. Two-tailed Mann–Whitney test (<em>p</em> value &lt; 2.2e-16). ****<em>p</em> ≤ 0.0001, ***<em>p</em> ≤ 0.001, **<em>p</em> ≤ 0.01, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05. Source data are provided with this paper. <strong>B</strong> Differences in cellular senescence secretory product score, calculated from the SenMayo gene set, between all cell types at 1G or simulated uG. 1G group’s (<em>n</em> = 11,934 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) median is 0.001, with min = −0.084 and max = 0.302; uG group’s (<em>n</em> = 16,568 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) median is 0.006, with min = −0.091 and max = 0.569. Two-tailed Mann–Whitney test (<em>p</em> value &lt; 2.2e-16). ****<em>p</em> ≤ 0.0001. Source data are provided with this paper. <strong>C</strong> Meta-transcriptome detection of mycobacteria, retrovirus, and total virus abundance in 1G (<em>n</em> = 11,934 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) and uG (<em>n</em> = 16,568 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) conditions. The bar plot shows the mean with an error bar representing the standard error of the mean (SEM). For <em>Mycobacterium canettii</em> (<em>p</em> value &lt; 0.0001), mean of 1G = 2.5e-4 ± 9.0e-6 and uG = 5.5e-4 ± 1.4e-5. For <em>Gammaretrovirus</em> (<em>p</em> value &lt; 0.0001), mean of 1G = 1.4e-6 ± 2.0e-7 and uG = 4.5e-6 ± 3.4e-7. For the total virus (<em>p</em> value = 0.0421), mean of 1G = 1.3e-5 ± 6.3e-7 and uG = 1.6e-5 ± 6.6e-7. Two-tailed Mann–Whitney test; *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05, ****<em>p</em> ≤ 0.0001. Source data are provided with this paper. (D to F) NicheNet predicted significant ligand-receptor interaction between total T cells (Receiver) and the antigen-presenting cells (Sender) as <strong>D</strong> B cells, <strong>E</strong> DCs, and <strong>F</strong> monocytes in uG vs 1G condition (i.e., induced in uG over 1G).</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par11">Finally, as we identified strong changes in gene expression pathways linked to innate cells, including those with the capacity to present antigen, we leveraged this knowledge to utilize NicheNet<sup><a href="#CR27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR27">27</a></sup> algorithms to generate a comprehensive predicted ligand:receptor interactome map of human antigen-presenting cells (APCs, plus plasmacytoid dendritic cells) and T cells in simulated microgravity vs 1G (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2D–F</a>). Across APC donors and recipient T cells, we identified numerous significantly predicted ligand-receptor interactions to be elevated in simulated microgravity vs 1G. For instance, monocytes and dendritic cells induced IL-1 proteins while some B cells provided IL-23A, and IL-7. All APCs provided unique chemokine signals to T cells. <em>Mmp9</em>, <em>ccl2,</em> and <em>thbs1</em> were amongst our most significantly induced genes in simulated microgravity, and the products of these genes show differential predicted receptor expression (e.g., CD44, CD47, ITGB1, CCR4, CCR5) in T cells (Supplementary Figs. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">4</a>–<a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">6</a>) but all show predicted enhanced target gene expression in T cells. Thus, while simulated microgravity itself likely induces direct transcriptional changes in immune cells, we cannot exclude local paracrine effects of secreted products from one immune cell to another also contributing to our overall gene expression and pathway changes.</p>
<p id="Par12">After stimulating PBMCs with a TLR7/8 agonist in 1G and simulated microgravity, we characterized 23 clusters of immune cells by UMAP (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3A</a>). In contrast to the unstimulated conditions, stimulation with a TLR7/8 agonist induced a robust preferential expansion of CD4<sup>+</sup> central memory (TCM) cells (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3B</a>). The microgravity itself impacted differential response to stimulation. Consistent with previous reports, simulated microgravity dampened expansion/responses of NK cells, and CD8<sup>+</sup> TEM cells to a lesser extent in the donors examined<sup><a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">18</a></sup>, as well as MAIT cell numbers, a cell type with previously unknown responses to microgravity. Simulated microgravity drove a preferential increase in CD14<sup>+</sup> monocytes over 1G controls, indicating that this cell type is especially sensitive to the combination of simulated microgravity and TLR7/8 activation.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig3"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 3. Simulated microgravity induces a distinct single-cell transcriptional landscape of human PBMCs following TLR7/8 stimulation.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig3_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/e12c90302a75/41467_2023_42013_Fig3_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1048" height="858" width="796" alt="Fig. 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> UMAP plot of TLR7/8 agonist stimulated (9 hours stimulation + 16 hours conditioning prior to stimulation = 25 hours total culture) PBMCs single-cell transcriptomics, pooled from a male (36 years old) and a female (25 years old) donor, that underwent either 1G or simulated uG. Cells were resolved into 23 distinct clusters. <strong>B</strong> Quantification of relative abundance of each cluster of single PBMCs by percentage, or log2FC between stimulated uG and 1G conditions. Source data are provided with this paper. <strong>C</strong> Volcano plot of DEGs across all immune cell types between TLR7/8 agonist simulated uG and 1G; DEGs (including log2FC and adj. <em>p</em>) were calculated by the MAST method. <em>P</em> values were adjusted by the B-H correction. Adj. <em>p</em> cutoff is 0.05, and log2FC cutoff is 0.25. <strong>D</strong> Dot plot showing the top DEGs from <strong>C</strong> and their expression levels across 19 immune cell populations. DEGs (including log2FC and adj. <em>p</em>) were calculated by the MAST method; <em>p</em> values were adjusted by the B-H correction. Spot color reflects log2FC of TLR7/8 agonist simulated uG vs 1G, while spot size shows −log10(adj. <em>p</em>). <strong>E</strong> UMAP of trajectory analysis of 1G and simulated uG TLR7/8 agonist stimulated PBMCs. Filled circle nomenclature was described in Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1E</a>. <strong>F</strong> Canonical pathway enrichment analysis obtained from IPA is shown across 19 immune cell clusters. Spot color reflects IPA z-score enrichment of TLR7/8 agonist-activated simulated uG vs 1G, with red meaning predicted activation of the pathway in simulated uG and blue meaning repression of the pathway in simulated uG. Spot size shows the level of significance via −log10 (adj. <em>p</em>). Adj. <em>p</em> was calculated by the Fisher’s Exact Test (right-tailed) followed by B-H adjustment.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par13">Across all cell types, the combination of simulated microgravity and TLR7/8 stimulation altered the expression of over 9000 differentially expressed genes (DEGs) with adj <em>P</em> cutoff of &lt;0.05 (Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM7" class="usa-link">4</a>). As with the unstimulated data, we refined this list to a core gene list of ~317 DEGs based on |log2FC| of &gt;0.2. This list was further reduced to visualize on a Volcano plot with |log2FC|&gt;0.25 (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3C</a>), showing only the most positively and negatively altered genes. Some of the most induced genes by simulated microgravity over 1G in response to TLR7/8 agonist included cytokines and chemokines, such as <em>ccl8</em>, <em>ccl4</em>, <em>ccl7</em>, <em>cxcl8</em>, and <em>il1b</em>, and acute response proteins like <em>s100a8</em>, <em>s100a9</em>, <em>s100a11</em>, and <em>thbs1</em>. Additional genes induced in simulated microgravity were linked to tryptophan breakdown (<em>ido1</em>), mitochondrial antioxidant defense (<em>sod2</em>), the cytoskeleton (<em>rhoq</em>), and iron storage genes like <em>fth1</em>, <em>ftl</em>. The most downregulated genes when comparing simulated microgravity to 1G during TLR7/8 stimulation, included genes belonging to guanylate binding proteins (<em>gbp1</em>, <em>gbp2</em>, <em>gbp4</em>, <em>gbp5</em>), which were the most reduced set of genes by fold change and adj P, as well as interferon pathway genes, like <em>irf1</em>, <em>stat1</em>, <em>isg20</em>, <em>ifi16</em>, cold shock genes (<em>rbm3</em>, <em>cirbp</em>), cell killing genes (<em>prf1, gzmb</em>) and T/NK cell activation markers like <em>cd69</em>. Many of these genes were consistently altered by simulated microgravity alone without stimulation, indicating a conserved response, even in the setting of additional exogenous stimulation with a TLR ligand. Expression of top DEGs across 19 populations of immune cells is shown in Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3D</a>. Volcano plots of DEGs for individual immune cell clusters are shown in Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">7</a>. CD14<sup>+</sup> monocytes, NK cells, CD8<sup>+</sup> TEM, and CD4<sup>+</sup> TCM cells showed the most significant changes in the top most altered genes induced by TLR7/8 agonist stimulation in simulated microgravity. Interestingly, using single-cell trajectory analysis (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3E</a>), we identified fewer trajectories in simulated microgravity stimulated with TLR7/8 compared to the 1G control. These findings suggest that under simulated microgravity, cells display reduced differentiation states in response to stimulation.</p>
<p id="Par14">IPA results (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3F</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM8" class="usa-link">5</a>) generated using our core list of approximately 317 genes from the overall populations, as well as the DEGs in major immune cell types (Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM9" class="usa-link">6</a>) demonstrated that nearly all immune cells show changes across numerous pathways during microgravity and TLR7/8 induction. Major pathways reduced across most immune cells in simulated microgravity included PKR in interferon response (and associated eif2 signaling), interferon signaling, JAK/STAT signaling, pyroptosis signaling, cytotoxic T cell mediated killing of target cells and death receptor signaling. Major pathways induced by short term simulated microgravity included sirtuin signaling, fibrosis signaling, signaling by Rho GTPases, BAG2 (heat-shock protein 70 interactor) signaling, HIF1α signaling, acute phase response and associated HMGB1 signaling, amongst others. These pathways are consistent with microgravity facilitating innate like inflammation at the expense of interferon driven adaptive immunity and adaptive immune effector function (e.g., CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell killing). Despite some similarities in pathways altered to simulated microgravity alone (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1F</a>), we actually detected a lower iAge score globally across all immune populations in simulated microgravity plus TLR7/8 compared to 1G controls (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4A</a>). While the reason for this finding is unclear, it appears to have been driven by a highly significant reduction in score by naive B cells, naive CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells, and reductions in lesser studied PBMC populations, hematopoietic stem and progenitor cells (HSPC)s and double negative T cells. Lower iAge also could be reflective of altered immune activation in simulated microgravity, such as seen in CD16<sup>+</sup> monocytes (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4A</a>, right panel). Despite a reduction in iAge, we still observed an increased SenMayo score in simulated PBMCs (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4B</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">8</a>), illustrating different compositions of genes in these two gene sets.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig4"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 4. Simulated microgravity induces predictive functional alterations in immune cells following TLR7/8 stimulation.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig4_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/e9fcfff19d3b/41467_2023_42013_Fig4_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1259" height="392" width="683" alt="Fig. 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig4/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> Differences in iAge index between all cell types (left) and across 19 individual immune cell types (right) after TLR7/8 agonist activation in 1G or simulated uG. Data are presented in the same way as described in Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2A</a>. 1G group (<em>n</em> = 14,916 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) is shown in blue and uG group (<em>n</em> = 12,230 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) is shown in yellow. The median for 1G group is 16.4, with min = 2.9 and max = 25.9. The median for uG is 13.2, with min = −1.7 and max = 23.9. Two-tailed Mann–Whitney test (<em>p</em> value &lt; 2.2e-16). ****<em>p</em> ≤ 0.0001, ***<em>p</em> ≤ 0.001, **<em>p</em> ≤ 0.01, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05. Source data are provided with this paper. <strong>B</strong> Differences in cellular senescence secretory product score, calculated from the SenMayo gene set, between all cell types with TLR7/8 agonist activated 1G or simulated uG. 1G group’s (<em>n</em> = 14,916 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) median is −0.008, with min = −0.099 and max = 0.588; uG group’s (<em>n</em> = 12,230 cells examined over 2 independent experiments) median is 0.022, with min = −0.095 and max = 0.551. Two-tailed Mann–Whitney test (<em>p</em> value &lt; 2.2e-16), ****<em>p</em> ≤ 0.0001. Source data are provided with this paper. <strong>C</strong>–<strong>E</strong> NicheNet predicted significant ligand-receptor interaction between total T cells (Receiver) and the antigen-presenting cells (Sender) as <strong>C</strong> B cells, <strong>D</strong> DCs, and <strong>E</strong> monocytes in TLR7/8 agonist activated simulated uG vs 1G condition (i.e., induced in uG over 1G).</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par15">NicheNet analysis across major APC types to T cells post TLR7/8 agonist in simulated microgravity vs 1G (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4C–E</a>, and Supplementary Figs. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">9</a>–<a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">11</a>) illustrated some of the significant cytokines, chemokines, surface molecule ligands and receptors used in simulated microgravity upon TLR7/8 stimulation. Compared to the unstimulated interactome (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2D–F</a>), we saw increased production and diversity of inflammatory cytokines and chemokines used. We again noted IL-1 produced in APCs, but also noted increased TNF superfamily products like TNF, TNFSF12 (TNF-related weak inducer of apoptosis, TWEAK) and TNFSF15 (vascular endothelial growth inhibitor, VEGI), and lymphotoxin (LTA) preferentially produced to modulate T cell function.</p>
<p id="Par16">Next, we assessed the differential responsiveness of immune cells to TLR7/8 stimulation (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">12</a> and Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM10" class="usa-link">7</a>). Under 1G conditions, stimulation led to a marked induction of CD4<sup>+</sup> TCM, at the expense of CD14<sup>+</sup> monocytes and CD4<sup>+</sup> naive T cells, coupled to an expected pronounced inflammatory gene signature, including marked induction of interferon inducible genes, <em>gbp</em> transcripts, and chemokines across most immune cell populations (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">12A–E</a>). Under simulated microgravity, TLR7/8 stimulation also induced CD4<sup>+</sup> TCM, at the expense of naive CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells, though proportions of CD14<sup>+</sup> monocyte populations did not reduce as seen in 1G (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">12F, G</a>). In simulated microgravity, TLR7/8 stimulation also induced a robust expression of inflammatory genes, including interferon inducing genes across most cell types (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">12H–J</a>). Next, to determine the sensitivity of individual immune cell populations to TLR7/8 agonist in 1G vs simulated microgravity, we compared the differences in responsiveness to stimulation. We subtracted the fold change induction in 1G from induction in simulated microgravity to determine sensitivity to stimulation. Remarkably, across overall immune cells, we see a pattern of reduced responsiveness to TLR7/8 simulation in simulated microgravity to most of the highest genes induced at 1G (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">13A</a>), with T cells and NK cells showing the most reduced inflammatory gene induction. T cells, NK cells, and overall across all cells exhibited blunting of induction of numerous genes in interferon signaling, and <em>gbp</em> genes in simulated microgravity in response to TLR7/8 agonist. Interestingly, monocytes tended to maintain such responses better in simulated microgravity, consistent with their predisposition to some inflammatory pathways in simulated microgravity. Some chemokines, such as <em>ccl3</em>, <em>ccl4</em>, <em>ccl8</em>, and <em>cxcl10</em> appeared to be induced better in simulated uG across overall immune cell populations, though monocytes actually showed reduced induction of some of these chemokines, likely due to their capacity to produce them in simulated microgravity without stimulation (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1D</a>). Nonetheless, the overall effects in sensitivity to stimulation in the “overall” category of immune cells largely followed the same pattern seen in the total magnitude of response of stimulated microgravity vs stimulated 1G (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3D</a>).</p>
<p id="Par17">Next, we sought to identify genes uniquely altered by simulated microgravity irrespective of stimulation, as well as genes unique to microgravity under stimulation. First, to identify unique genes, regardless of stimulation, altered by simulated microgravity, we plotted out the most significant DEGs by the absolute sum of fold change under both stimulated and unstimulated conditions (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">13B</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM11" class="usa-link">8</a>). Across overall immune cells, regardless of stimulation, we still identified conserved increases in chemokines and acute response factors, coupled to reduced <em>gbp</em> expression and other interferon genes (e.g., <em>irf1</em>, <em>stat1</em>) imparted by simulated microgravity. Next, we mapped out the overall overlap of gene signatures and common genes between post-stimulation in 1G vs simulated microgravity (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">13C–E</a> and Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM12" class="usa-link">9</a>), and found that microgravity imparts a number of unique genes to TLR ligation that are not seen in 1G. Overall, these findings identify core-conserved DEGs specifically sensitive to simulated microgravity as well as unique signatures to simulated microgravity.</p>
<p id="Par18">Finally, we assessed if sex plays a role in the magnitude of response to simulated microgravity. In the unstimulated state, female cells showed only slightly more DEGs induced, while the male cells had slightly more genes reduced (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">14A</a>). Male NK cells and monocytes were more sensitive to microgravity while female B cells showed more sensitivity than male B cells. Upon stimulation, male cells overall were more sensitive to simulated microgravity, especially in having more downregulated DEGs (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">14B</a>). Volcano plots of DEGs across all cell types between the female and male are shown in Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">14C–F</a> and Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM13" class="usa-link">10</a>. In both male and female cells, acute phase response and inflammatory genes like <em>mmp9</em>, <em>ccl2</em>, <em>s100a8</em>, and <em>thbs1</em> were among the most induced genes, while reduced interferon regulators like <em>stat1</em> and reduced cold shock genes like <em>rbm3</em> and <em>cirbp</em> were consistently downregulated in both sexes in simulated microgravity. Upon stimulation, both sexes again show increases in the total magnitude of acute inflammatory, reactive oxygen species (ROS)-related, and acute phase genes like chemokines, <em>thbs1</em>, <em>mmp9</em>, <em>ncf1,</em> and <em>sod2</em> in simulated microgravity coupled to reduced interferon, <em>gbp</em>s, cold shock, and some ribosomal protein genes in simulated microgravity. Many of these changes are reflected in IPA pathway analysis by sex (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">14G</a>) and many of these core features were also conserved when data from sexes were pooled (Figs. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1F</a>, <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3F</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Single-cell validation identifies core features of immune dysfunction in microgravity and spaceflight</h3>
<p id="Par19">Next, to better validate conserved genes and pathways from our single-cell signatures without TLR7/8 agonist in simulated microgravity, we compared our core signature of 375 DEGs against additional datasets. First, we repeated experiments in a validation cohort of freshly isolated PBMCs from young donors (<em>n</em> = 6, age range 20–46), and spun these PBMCs for 25 hours, prior to performing bulk RNA-seq analysis. Using CIBERSORTx<sup><a href="#CR28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR28">28</a></sup>, we first mapped predicted changes in population frequency and were able to confirm increased CD14<sup>+</sup> monocyte frequencies in simulated microgravity, consistent with our single-cell data (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">15</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM14" class="usa-link">11</a>). Between validation samples spun in simulated microgravity vs 1G controls, we identified 2149 genes differentially expressed (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5A</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM15" class="usa-link">12</a>). Despite the variability of data inherent to bulk RNA-seq of different populations of cells between donors, we still saw a highly significant correlation in normalized gene counts per specific gene between datasets both at 1G and in simulated microgravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5B</a>). Moreover, we identified overlap in over 28% of our core signature genes (same directionality) across all immune cells from our single-cell analysis (106/375 = 28.3%) (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5C</a>). Many of the overlapping genes induced were consistent with our most robustly altered core pathways from the single-cell data. For instance, we saw shared overlapping genes induced in acute immune responses (such as <em>s100a8</em>, <em>s100a12</em>, <em>thbs1, il1b</em>), chemokines (like <em>cxcl8</em>), heat-shock proteins (<em>hsp90aa1</em>, <em>hspa1a</em>, <em>hspb1</em>), autophagy (<em>atg7</em>) and the actin cytoskeleton (<em>rhou</em>). Overlapping reduced genes in simulated microgravity, like in our single-cell datasets, included interferon response (<em>stat1, irf1</em>) and associated guanylate binding proteins (<em>gbp1</em>, <em>gbp2</em>, <em>gbp4, gbp5</em>), and cold shock genes (<em>rbm3</em>), amongst others. Overall, there was a highly significant enrichment and over representation of our core single-cell DEGs across our bulk validation cohort by Fisher’s Exact Test for gene overlap (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5C</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig5"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 5. Validation of single-cell signatures identifies overlapping features of immune dysfunction in simulated microgravity and spaceflight.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig5_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/58296f957de5/41467_2023_42013_Fig5_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1595" height="858" width="794" alt="Fig. 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig5/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> Volcano plot of DEGs from simulated uG vs.1G (25 hours) Bulk RNA-seq. Genes that are consistently upregulated across single-cell and bulk sequencing are labeled in red; genes that are consistently downregulated across the two datasets are labeled in blue. DEGs (including log2FC and p value) were calculated by the DESeq2 method; <em>p</em> value was determined by two-tailed Wald test and adjusted by the B-H method. Data were obtained from PBMCs from 3 male (ages 37, 22, 32 years old) and 3 female (age 27, 26, 40 years old) donors. <strong>B</strong> Spearman correlation of normalized counts between single-cell and bulk RNA-seq from simulated uG (<em>R</em> = 0.82, <em>p</em> &lt; 2.2e-16) and 1G (<em>R</em> = 0.8, <em>p</em> &lt; 2.2e-16) conditions. Two-tailed <em>p</em> value. <strong>C</strong> Venn diagram summarizing the overlapping DEGs between single-cell (SC; adj. <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05, log2FC &gt; |0.1|) and bulk RNA-seq (Bulk; <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) simulated uG vs. 1G. DEGs that are upregulated in both datasets are listed in the red box; DEGs that are downregulated in both datasets are listed in the blue box. The overlapping <em>p</em> value was calculated by the Fisher’s Exact Test, right-tailed. <strong>D</strong> Volcano plot of DEGs from Flight (ISS 33 days, <em>n</em> = 10) vs. Ground (<em>n</em> = 10) mouse spleen bulk RNA-seq (GLDS-420). Genes that are consistently upregulated across single-cell human PBMCs and bulk mouse spleen RNA-seq are labeled in red; genes that are consistently downregulated across the two are labeled in blue. DEGs (including log2FC and adj. <em>p</em>) were calculated by the DESeq2 method; <em>p</em> value was determined by two-tailed Wald test and adjusted by the B-H method. <strong>E</strong> Venn diagram summarizing the overlapping DEGs between human PBMCs single-cell (SC; adj. <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05, log2FC &gt; |0.1|) simulated uG vs. 1G and the mouse orthologous DEGs from Flight vs. Ground spleen bulk RNA-seq (GLDS-420; <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05). DEGs that are upregulated in both datasets are listed in the red box; DEGs that are downregulated in both datasets are listed in the blue box. The overlapping <em>p</em> value (5.4e-14) was calculated by the Fisher’s Exact Test, right-tailed. <strong>F</strong> Heatmap of overlapping DEGs between human PBMCs simulated uG vs 1G and the I4 mission (<em>n</em> = 4) post-flight (R + 1) vs preflight (L-44) dataset. Both datasets are single-cell RNA-seq with DEGs defined by adj. <em>p</em> value &lt; 0.05 and log2FC &gt; |0.1|. Genes that are consistently upregulated across single-cell human PBMCs and I4 datasets are labeled in dark red (left). Genes that are consistently downregulated across datasets are labeled in dark blue (right). Genes that significantly overlap, but show reversal in their expression directions are labeled in gray. The overlapping <em>p</em> value (1e-200) was calculated by the Fisher’s Exact Test, right-tailed. <strong>G</strong> Heatmap of IPA canonical pathways enriched from DEGs between human PBMCs SC (single-cell RNA-seq uG vs 1G) and I4 (<em>n</em> = 4, R + 1 vs L-44). Enriched pathways have B-H adjusted <em>p</em> values &lt; 0.05 (−log10(adj. p)&gt;1.3). Red indicates a predicted activation in pathways, whereas blue indicates a predicted inhibition in pathways.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par20">We next sought to validate overlapping genes against mice and people flown in LEO. While multiple stressors exist in LEO, the proximity to Earth and the presence of the Earth’s magnetic field negates some effects of galactic cosmic rays, especially at the altitude of the ISS. Thus, microgravity plays an important role in driving phenotypic changes in LEO. To accomplish this goal, we first mined data from NASA’s GeneLab database for its largest study looking at a major immune organ, the spleen, in mice flown on the ISS. The GLDS-420 study provides data from the spleens of ten mice housed on the ISS for 33 days compared to ten ground controls. Though this cohort represents longer exposure to microgravity than our single-cell data’s more acute exposure, any overlapping genes could represent persistent microgravity-sensitive immune cell genes across longer duration exposure. From the GLDS-420 dataset, we identified 1448 significant DEGs (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5D</a>), of which 50/375 (13.3%) overlapped in the same direction as our single-cell core list (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5E</a>). Interestingly, many of the overlapping genes were represented as part of altered core pathways from the single-cell data. For instance, we saw shared induced overlapping genes in acute immune responses or complement (such as <em>c3</em>), autophagy (<em>atg7</em>), heat-shock responses (<em>hsp90ab1</em>, <em>hsp90aa1</em>, <em>hspa1a</em>, <em>hspa1</em>), and the cytoskeleton (<em>dynll1</em>). Overlapping reduced genes included interferon response (<em>stat1</em>), and again, cold shock genes (<em>cirbp</em>, <em>rbm3</em>), amongst others. Overall, we saw a significant enrichment in our core single-cell DEGs across mouse spleens flown in space by Fisher’s Exact Test for gene overlap (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5E</a>). Pathway analysis with IPA was next performed to identify major canonical pathways altered across all four complete datasets (single-cell unstimulated, single-cell stimulated with TLR7/8 agonist, Bulk RNA-seq validation unstimulated, and GLDS-420), including overlapping pathways shared across all datasets. These pathways are displayed in a heat map for comparison (see below), and will be described at that point.</p>
<p id="Par21">To better translate the usefulness of our single-cell atlas to human spaceflight, we compared our core list of 375 DEGs across single cells in simulated microgravity to changes across all single cells from the Inspiration Four (I4) crew members. The I4 mission provides a compelling comparison since crew members spent up to three days in LEO, a timeline not too different from our 25-hour time point. Moreover, I4 gene lists were also generated by single-cell sequencing, making it a comparable technology for our analysis. However, it is important to note that the I4 datasets contain a few important caveats. First, the altitude flown by I4 crew (585 km/364miles) predispose the astronauts to higher radiation exposures than what would typically be experienced on the ISS (408 km/254miles altitude). Additionally, the I4 datasets were derived from PBMC gene expression comparisons between post-flight (1 day after Return/R + 1 in our case) vs preflight (44 days before launch/L-44 in our case). Since the changes from the I4 single-cell data represent changes encompassing effects of spaceflight, plus return to the ground, including short-term exposures to hyper-gravity, and one day of return to 1 g gravity (all which manifest as increased gravity exposure to inflight conditions), we considered overlapping immune cell genes in either direction on return to be gravity sensitive genes. Remarkably, despite these caveats, we found a very robust overlap of nearly 60% of DEGs in PBMCs in simulated microgravity (210/375 = 56%) to be also significantly altered across all immune cells in the I4 mission (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5F</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM16" class="usa-link">13</a>). Of these significantly altered genes across I4 data, 122 were altered in the same direction as I4 data, and 88 in the opposite direction. To gain a better understanding of the pathways and mechanisms impacted by gravity and spaceflight in the immune system, we compared pathways between simulated microgravity and the entire I4 dataset (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5G</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM17" class="usa-link">14</a>, and Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">16A</a>). While we consider all of these pathways to be potentially gravity sensitive, we considered pathways altered in the opposite directions to be potentially acutely sensitive to gravity, while those pathways altered in the same direction likely take longer to normalize from a microgravity environment upon return.</p>
<p id="Par22">Both simulated microgravity and the I4 mission pathway results indicated reduced T-cell effector subset development, reduced oxidative phosphorylation, and increased pathways associated with innate immunity (e.g., Coronavirus pathogenesis, FcR phagocytosis in monocytes, cytokine storm, chemokine signaling, ROS production in macrophages), as well as hypoxia and glycolytic metabolism (HIF1α signaling) and cell stress (e.g., sirtuin signaling). Interestingly, the return to gravity seen in the I4 mission reversed reductions in natural killer cell signaling and reversed pathways linked to poor adaptive immunity like IL-15 signaling, suggesting these pathways may be sensitive to acute changes in gravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5G</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM17" class="usa-link">14</a>). From the I4 dataset, we also noticed a consistent reduction in ribosomal subunit genes in the I4 data (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5F</a>), which might be reflective of a stress response and reduced protein translation upon return to earth), and only some of these genes were reduced in simulated microgravity. Consistently, there was a marked reduction in EIF2 signaling in the provided I4 DEGs. Across all pathways, regardless of direction, we noticed many pathways pertaining to the cytoskeleton or to a mechanical extracellular environment (e.g., fibrosis, RAC, Rho family GTPases, RHOA, integrin signaling, leukocyte extravasation signaling, healing signaling etc.) to be altered by simulated microgravity or by spaceflight on immune cells.</p>
<p id="Par23">Next, to look for overlapping genes relevant to longer exposures to human spaceflight, we compared our core simulated microgravity signature of immune cells against available data from the JAXA Cell-Free Epigenome study in LEO (GLDS-530) and the Twins study<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>. For the JAXA mission, we had access to cell-free RNA data, which can sometimes give insight into changes to PBMCs, amongst other cells<sup><a href="#CR29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR29">29</a></sup>. During this mission, blood was sampled from 6 astronauts, with data pooled into a single count, at days 5, 30, 60, 120 post launch. Given that cell-free RNA is not a fully ideal comparison to RNA-seq from isolated PBMCs, we focused only on the two early time points, 5 days and 30 days post launch, because we saw significant overlap between our single-cell data in both the three-day I4 mission and in the 33-day GLDS-420 dataset. Thus, we compared 5 days and 30 days in-flight vs preflight differentially altered cell free RNA signatures for any possible overlap with our single-cell data in simulated microgravity. While we did not observe much overlap in our core 375 immune gene signature at 5 days (less than 10 genes), we did see significant overlap by 30 days (42/375 = 11.2% overlap in the same direction) (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">16B</a>). Interestingly, it was observed that cell-free RNA levels generally decreased across most genes in flight. Consequently, we hypothesized that identifying genes exhibiting increased expression could be particularly important for identifying over-represented processes. Remarkably, we did note that the most significantly elevated gene at 30 days in-flight vs preflight was <em>cdc42</em>, a key modulator of the cytoskeleton, as well as <em>dynll1</em> a dynein gene that was also upregulated in our single-cell analysis.</p>
<p id="Par24">We next reclustered our single-cell data (Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM18" class="usa-link">15</a>) to compare the DEGs in our equivalent single-cell populations with those obtained from sorted CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells, CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells, B cells, and lymphocyte-depleted immune cells from the NASA Twins study, which compares in-flight vs ground twin control<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>. The Twins study provides intriguing data on the impact of LEO on the immune system, but has caveats in that exposure to LEO was calculated in only a single individual through bulk RNA-seq, and at multiple time points across one full year in space, a different duration than our shorter gene sets. Nonetheless, compared to our reclustered CD4<sup>+</sup>, CD8<sup>+</sup>, and CD19<sup>+</sup> gene sets, we found significant overlap in some genes comparing the effects of simulated microgravity to spaceflight (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">17A–C</a>). Across multiple cell types, we saw changes in genes involved in redox regulation (e.g., reduced <em>txnip</em>), and in genes involved in interferon responses (e.g., reduced <em>stat1</em> and <em>gbp5</em>). Interestingly, we saw significantly reduced cold shock gene, <em>cirbp</em>, a similar functioning gene to <em>rbm3</em>, in B cells in space. In our lymphocyte depleted (i.e., myeloid) recluster, we saw a large and highly significant overlap of about 163 genes with the Twins lymphocyte-depleted bulk RNA-seq data (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">17D</a>). Many of the overlapping genes induced by simulated microgravity or spaceflight included genes involved in innate immunity and inflammation (e.g., <em>il1b</em>, <em>s100a12</em>, <em>thbs1</em> etc.), the cytoskeleton (<em>rhoq</em>, <em>rhou</em>), and hypoxia signaling (e.g., <em>hif1α</em>). Some interesting downregulated genes in both datasets in myeloid cells included again <em>gbp5</em>, <em>cirbp</em>, <em>txnip</em> like seen in T and B cells from the Twins study. We also noted a number of overlapping downregulated genes in antigen presentation (e.g., <em>tap1</em>, <em>tap2</em>, <em>hla-e</em>, <em>hla-dp1a</em>, etc). IPA analysis on this data largely captured the increases in innate immune inflammatory pathways, including increases in fibrosis signaling, IL-6 signaling, acute phase response, cytokine storm, and HIF1α signaling seen across some of our previous datasets (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">17E</a>, Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM19" class="usa-link">16</a>). Overall, these data enforce the idea of classically activated basal myeloid inflammatory changes in microgravity and spaceflight.</p>
<p id="Par25">Given that many of our altered pathways in simulated microgravity involved predicted mitochondrial dysfunction and/or the cytoskeleton, we used Airyscan super-resolution confocal microscopy to characterize immune cell mitochondrial and actin morphological networks to look for alterations in simulated microgravity compared to 1G controls. Interestingly, while 25 hours of simulated microgravity did not alter mean cell area across PBMCs, it did alter actin granularity parameters, as well as intensity and variance, consistent with cytoskeletal changes in acute simulated microgravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6A–C</a>), though these differences are mostly subtle to the naked eye. Using three-dimensional (3D) super-resolution imaging, 25 hours of simulated microgravity did not alter cell or nucleus volume, or nucleus shape, but it increased mean cell surface area and actin spike length, and decreased sphericity of the cells across PBMCs (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6A, B, D</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">18A, B</a>). Remarkably, 1G immune cells and simulated microgravity immune cells demonstrate unique spectral changes to actin rearrangement post TLR stimulation, such that TLR stimulation resulted in a different pattern of actin granularity spectral change in 1G compared to stimulation in simulated microgravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6C</a>). The effect of microgravity on the cytoskeleton in unstimulated immune cells was similar to the effect of TLR activation in 1G. During TLR stimulation in simulated microgravity, immune cells followed a unique dynamic actin rearrangement pattern, potentially even reversing the pattern observed in 1G with TLR stimulation. These results suggest that simulated microgravity itself may induce immune cytoskeleton alterations, which may mimic aspects of TLR ligation on the cytoskeleton. Short-term exposure to simulated microgravity showed some increases in the mitochondrial MitoTracker Red staining intensity and variance in the unstimulated conditions, without changes to fiber length, size, or volume (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">18C</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig6"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 6. Simulated microgravity induces distinct modifications to immune cell cytoskeletal morphology and cytokine production.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig6_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/9214d1e181be/41467_2023_42013_Fig6_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1920" height="445" width="677" alt="Fig. 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig6/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> Super-resolution microscopy analysis of actin in 2D for cell area (left), intensity (middle), and texture as punctate over diffuse index (PDI, variance/mean, right) between 25 hours of 1G or simulated uG. Dots represent individual PBMCs (<em>n</em> = 159 cells for 1G, and <em>n</em> = 154 cells for uG) from 4 independent donors. Donors were male (25 years old), and females (35, 38, and 46 years old). One outlier for actin intensity and actin PDI from each condition is removed based on Grubbs’ test. Two-tailed Welch’s <em>t</em> test was used for all comparisons. ***<em>p</em> ≤ 0.001. Data are plotted as mean ± standard error of the mean (SEM) and source data are provided with this paper. <strong>B</strong> Representative super-resolution microscopy images (2D left, 3D right) of PBMCs from 1G and simulated uG (25 hours) from donor 1 (35 yr F) and donor 2 (25 yr M; 2 of total 4 donors from <strong>A</strong> are shown here). 3D images better highlight changes to overall cell shape and actin protrusions in simulated uG. Scale bar = 2 μm and 1 μm, respectively. <strong>C</strong> Sixteen-channel granularity spectrum measurement of PBMCs stimulated with TLR7/8 agonist (9 hours stimulation, 16 hours conditioning prior to stimulation) from 1G (pink line) and uG (brown line) minus the corresponding unstimulated cells (25 hours total culture). The effect of simulated microgravity on unstimulated granularity spectrum is plotted in gray. Asterisks compare pink vs brown lines only. <em>P</em> values generated from unpaired two-tailed <em>t</em> test. <em>n</em> = 3 donors tested from <strong>A</strong>, 35-year-old female sample was not used. **<em>p</em> ≤ 0.01, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05. Data are plotted as mean ± SEM, and source data are provided with this paper. <strong>D</strong> Super-resolution microscopy analysis of 3D actin surface area (left, 1G <em>n</em> = 179 cells and uG <em>n</em> = 194 cells) and actin spike length (right, 1G <em>n</em> = 162 cells and uG <em>n</em> = 165 cells) between 25 hours of 1G or simulated uG. Dots represent individual PBMCs from 4 independent donors. Donors were male (25 years old), and females (35, 38, and 46 years old). Two-tailed Welch’s <em>t</em> test was used to calculate <em>p</em> values. *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05, **<em>p</em> ≤ 0.01. Data are plotted as mean ± SEM and source data are provided with this paper. <strong>E</strong> G-LISA levels of active GTP-bound Cdc42 in PBMCs either unstimulated (25 hours) or treated with TLR7/8 agonist (9 hours + 16 hours conditioning) from 1G and simulated uG. <em>n</em> = 7, donors were male (25 years old), and females (38, 46, 25, 27, 26, and 40 years old). Two-tailed paired parametric <em>t</em> test was used to calculate <em>p</em> values, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05, ***<em>p</em> ≤ 0.001. Data are plotted as mean ± SEM and source data are provided with this paper. <strong>F</strong> ELISA levels of secreted IFNs by PBMCs treated with TLR7/8 agonist (16 hours conditioning + 9 hours stimulation) from 1G and simulated uG. <em>n</em> = 9, donors were male (36 years old), and females (33, 25, 38, 46, 27, 25, 26, and 40 years old). Two-tailed paired parametric <em>t</em> test was used, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05. Data are plotted as mean ± SEM, and source data are provided with this paper. <strong>G</strong> ELISA level of secreted ILs by PBMCs exposed to 25 hours simulated uG and 1G. <em>n</em> = 10 for IL-8 and <em>n</em> = 11 for IL-6, donors were females (32, 25, 38, 46, 25, 27, 26, 40 years old) and males (36, 33, 26 years old); 38-year-old female sample was not used for IL-8. Two-tailed paired parametric <em>t</em> test was used, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05. Data are plotted as mean ± SEM and source data are provided with this paper.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par26">Since we detected morphological changes to the actin network, as well as noting changes in multiple altered cytoskeleton-related pathways across multiple datasets, including in the pathways “Signaling by Rho Family GTPases” or “regulation of actin-based motility by Rho”, we next sought to screen for active GTP-bound Rho GTPases, Rac1, RhoA, and Cdc42, using G-LISA technology<sup><a href="#CR30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR30">30</a></sup> across further batches of isolated paired PBMCs. After 25 hours of simulated microgravity, regardless of stimulation conditions, we saw elevated levels of active GTP-bound Cdc42, consistent with cytoskeleton mobilization and the increase in actin spikes (indicative of filopodia)<sup><a href="#CR31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR31">31</a></sup> observed due to simulated microgravity (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6E</a>). Active GTP-Rac1 was not altered at baseline in simulated microgravity, though showed a trend to induction with TLR7/8 stimulation (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">18D, E</a>). Levels of active GTP-RhoA were low in our samples, but trended lower in simulated microgravity without stimulation, and higher under stimulation, analogous to our single-cell data predictions (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">18D, E</a>). Overall, these data suggest that simulated microgravity changes some Rho GTPase activity consistent with our transcriptional data, though ultimate impacts on cytoskeleton shape, variance, and dynamics likely involve additional contributing factors, including possibly other Rho GTPase family members not assessed.</p>
<p id="Par27">We next sought to investigate our core signature of reduced IFN signaling elicited in microgravity across immune cells (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6F</a>, Supplemental Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">19A, B</a>). Specifically, we assessed whether reduced interferon signaling was due to reduced local production of interferons. Supernatants from 25 hours unstimulated or 9 hours R848 simulated (25 hours total culture) PBMCs were assessed by ELISA for total IFNα (detecting 12 IFNα subtypes), and IFNγ. Simulated microgravity significantly reduced both IFNα and IFNγ secretion with TLR7/8 stimulation. At baseline, the levels of these cytokines were low, and variable, and thus not significantly different between 1G and simulated microgravity. These findings point to reduced production of IFNs in simulated microgravity, at least under TLR stimulation, as measured by ELISA, potentially as one contributing mechanism to reduced interferon signaling observed at the transcriptional level.</p>
<p id="Par28">Finally, to functionally validate how simulated microgravity impacts overall immune cell cytokine production, with and without TLR7/8 stimulation, across many cytokines simultaneously, we performed a 48-plex Luminex assay on cytokines secreted by PBMCs from 12 donors (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">19C, D</a>). Consistent with our single-cell and bulk RNA sequencing data, simulated microgravity was associated with increased or trending increases in mainly innate/monocyte immune cell-derived inflammatory cytokines and chemokines (e.g., IL-6, IL-8, IL-12p40, CCL4), coupled to a reduction in cytokines that associate with T cell activation or proliferation (e.g., IL-2, IL-7, IL-15). Concurrently, the Luminex results showed a significant IFNγ and a trending IFNα2 reduction upon TLR7/8 agonist stimulation in simulated microgravity, consistent with our above ELISA data (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6F</a>). IL-1, commonly induced in our sequencing data, also appeared elevated in simulated microgravity, though it exhibited high variability, precluding significance in the unstimulated state. In the stimulated state, IL-1β was significantly increased in simulated microgravity by Luminex analysis. Given the overlapping similarities between cytokines in the Luminex data and sequencing data for IL-1β, IL-6, and IL-8, we further assessed these cytokines by ELISA validation. Both IL-6 and IL-8 showed significant or near-significant increases by ELISA in simulated microgravity, while IL-1β demonstrated a trending increase (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6G</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">19E</a>). Upon stimulation, simulated microgravity further facilitated near-significant increases in IL-1β and IL-8 as validated by ELISA (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">19F</a>).</p>
<p id="Par29">To better understand how certain cell populations respond to TLR7/8 stimulation in simulated microgravity, we further validated key cytokines, IL-1β, IL-6, and IFNγ, by intracellular flow cytometry in monocyte, NK, and T cell subsets exposed to simulated microgravity compared to 1G conditions (Supplementary Figs. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">20</a>, <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">21</a>). Consistent with Luminex and ELISA data, we saw increased IL-1β production across all characterized monocyte populations (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">21A, B</a>). Interestingly, despite no overall differences in IL-6 by Luminex or ELISA in simulated microgravity to TLR7/8 stimulation, we still detected significant increases in a subset of monocytes only, as well as in NK cells (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">21C, E</a>). Despite increased cytokine production, we did not detect increases in the activation marker, HLA-DR, in monocyte populations (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">21D</a>). NK cells also showed a reduction in the proportion producing IFNγ, as well as reduced proportions of expression in the activation marker, CD69, and degranulation marker, LAMP-1, consistent with reduced functionality and response to stimulation in simulated microgravity (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">21F, G</a>). T cell subsets were less altered, though we still detected near significant or significant reductions in the proportions of CD4<sup>+</sup> and CD8<sup>+</sup> central memory T cells expressing activation marker, CD69, and in effector memory CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells expressing proliferation marker, Ki67 (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">21H</a>). Taken together with our ELISA and Luminex data, these findings demonstrate that simulated microgravity, alone or in the presence of TLR7/8 agonist, can functionally alter cytokine production across immune cells. In general, consistent with sequencing data, the features demonstrate monocyte inflammatory function coupled to impaired T cell and NK cell functionality in simulated microgravity. Thus, changes in cytokine signaling observed in simulated microgravity may occur at least in part to changes in upstream cytokine production.</p></section><section id="Sec5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Reversing simulated microgravity effects on the immune system</h3>
<p id="Par30">We have characterized multiple genes and pathways altered by simulated microgravity in the immune system; however, whether there are specific drugs or supplements that can directly target microgravity effects on immune cells is poorly characterized. Thus, we have utilized an in-house compound-gene interactome machine learning technology (Gene Compound Enrichment Analysis, GCEA), building on the HyperFoods model<sup><a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a></sup>, for the identification of drugs and food supplements that significantly map to altered genes in a dataset. Overall, our pipeline assesses &gt;2 million interactions between genes, drugs, and foods, across DrugBank, LINCS, and FoodDB<sup><a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a></sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7A</a>). Using these algorithms across our core signature of 375 DEGs altered by simulated microgravity across the immune system, we identified 115 compounds with adj <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05, and 474 compounds with <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05 that significantly map to our signature (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7B</a> and Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM20" class="usa-link">17</a>). Figure <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7B</a> shows the top 50 most significantly overlapping compounds to enriched DEGs. We next chose one compound, quercetin, based on its widespread availability for future travelers to space, and for its prominence as an anti-aging supplement, to validate whether it can reverse transcriptional insults of microgravity on the immune system. PBMCs (donors from the Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5A</a> cohort) were subjected to 25 hours in simulated microgravity, with or without quercetin (50 μM), for bulk RNA-seq analysis.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig7"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 7. Reversing simulated microgravity effects on the immune system with quercetin.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166937_41467_2023_42013_Fig7_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1dee/11166937/d609c6ed9f37/41467_2023_42013_Fig7_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e2097" height="1016" width="762" alt="Fig. 7"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig7/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>A</strong> Pipeline of microgravity and gene interacting compounds from discovery to validation. <strong>B</strong> Heatmap of top 50 simulated uG altered gene to compound interaction candidates. Compounds are listed on the right, and the predicted interacting genes are listed at the bottom. The color indicates the STITCH confidence score for compound-gene interaction. <strong>C</strong> Quercetin (50 μM) reverses the core gene expression signatures in simulated uG (25 hours). Log2FC levels of 106 core DEGs from simulated uG vs. 1G are plotted side-by-side to quercetin-treated uG vs. 1G in the heatmap. Red indicates positive log2FC, and blue indicates negative log2FC. 70% of the genes are reversed after quercetin treatment. The scatter plot below shows a negative association (Pearson correlation <em>R</em> = −0.35, <em>p</em> &lt; 0.001) between the log2FC levels of the 106 core genes from simulated uG vs. 1G and quercetin-treated uG vs. 1G. <strong>D</strong> Gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) shows the reversal effect of quercetin on the 106 core DEGs plotted in <strong>C</strong> heatmap. Quercetin treatment inverts the enrichment score (ES) in the upregulated core genes (from 0.8 to −0.64) and increases the ES of the downregulated core genes (from −0.75 to −0.55). All <em>p</em> values are &lt;0.0001. <strong>E</strong> IPA Canonical pathways altered by quercetin. Heatmap plots the comparison of quercetin-treated samples against non-treated bulk RNA-seq controls, single-cell TLR7/8 agonist stimulated and unstimulated samples, and GLDS-420 mouse spleens in space. Red indicates a predicted activation in pathways, whereas blue indicates a predicted inhibition in pathways. The datasets were clustered by quercetin treatment into 2 major groups via complete linkage hierarchical clustering method. <strong>F</strong> Quercetin reduces senescence and age-associated inflammatory gene outputs. Both SenMayo scores and iAge index are reduced in the quercetin-treated group with <em>p</em> value of 0.0746 and 0.0268 respectively. Compared with the untreated group, quercetin downregulates more senescence-related and age-associated inflammatory genes (from ↓48.8% to ↓63.2%). <em>n</em> = 6, donors were 3 males (age 37, 22, 32 years old) and 3 females (age 27, 26, 40 years old). Two-tailed paired <em>t</em> test, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05. <strong>G</strong> Quercetin (25 hours treatment) reduces ROS levels measured by 2’,7’-dichlorofluorescin diacetate (DCFDA) assay. <em>n</em> = 6 for 1G vs uG, donors were males (32, 37, and 38 years old) and females (34, 32, 37 years old). 34 yrs and 32 yrs female samples were not treated with quercetin, resulting in <em>n</em> = 4 for comparisons between 1G vs 1G+Quercetin and uG vs uG+ Quercetin. Two-tailed paired parametric <em>t</em> test was used, *<em>p</em> ≤ 0.05.
